Loud and Quiet Recitation: Wajib or Sunnah?
Ruling on loud and quiet recitation in prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on jahr and israr, cite the Quranic evidence, and explain why the ruling is wajib based on the Prophet's continuous practice.

Core Idea
Jahr and israr are wajib in prayer; omitting them deliberately requires sajdat al-sahw.

When you pray, do you have to pray aloud or quietly? The Hanafi school says it's not optional – it's wajib. This lesson unpacks the evidence from the Quran and the Prophet's practice, and explains why the school holds this position over the alternative view that it is merely sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what exactly is being ruled on: jahr (loud recitation) in Fajr, Maghrib, 'Isha', Jumu'ah, and 'Id prayers; israr (quiet recitation) in Zuhr and 'Asr. The hukm is wajib according to the Hanafi school.
The passage also mentions an alternate view that it is sunnah.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary evidence is Quran 17:110, which instructed the Prophet to recite aloud at night and quietly during the day. The Prophet's continuous practice (muwazabah) is also cited as proof.
The verse is qat'i al-thubut but zanni al-dalalah, so it is used in combination with sunnah.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The verse commands a middle path, but the Prophet's application differentiated by time. His consistent practice (muwazabah) indicates obligation because he never abandoned it throughout his life.
Hanafi usul holds that continuous practice of the Prophet indicates wujub.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some Hanafi scholars (and non-Hanafis) hold that jahr and israr are sunnah. Their reasoning: the purpose is recitation itself, and the manner is secondary. Thus, omission does not require sajdat al-sahw.
The passage refutes this by comparing to raml in tawaf – a practice that remained even after its original reason disappeared.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that jahr and israr are wajib. This is supported by Al-Marghinani in Al-Hidayah and the author of al-Mustakhlas. The evidence from the Prophet's continuous practice is deemed stronger.
Authority weight: zahir al-riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجهر والإسرار وقيل هما سنتان حتى لا يجب سجود السهو بتركهما؛ لأنهما ليسا بمقصودين، وإنما المقصود القراءة، فصارا كالقومة والدليل على الوجوب أن القراءة من أركان الصلاة، وهي: في الفرائض تؤدى على سبيل الشهرة لا الإخفاء، ولهذا كان النبي عليه يجهر في الصلوات كلها في ابتداء الأمر إلى أن قصد الكفار أن لا يسمعوا القرآن، وكانوا يلعبون فيه، فأنزل الله تعالى: ﴿وَلَا تَجْهَرْ بِصَلَاتِكَ وَلَا تُخَافِتْ بِهَا وَابْتَغِ بَيْنَ ذَلِكَ سَبِيلا﴾ (الإسراء:۱۱۰) بأن تجهر بصلاة الليل وتخافت بصلاة النهار؛ لأنهم كانوا مستعدين للأذى في صلاة النهار، وكان يجهر في العيدين والجمعة؛ لأن إقامتهما كانت في المدينة بجمع، وما كان للكفار حينئذ قوة للأذى، ثم وإن زال هذا العذر بقي الحكم كالرمل في الطواف؛ ولأنه عليه واظب عليهما في جميع عمره، فكانا واجباً. (فتح، مستخلص)
And loud recitation and quiet recitation, and it is said that both are sunnah, so that sajdat al-sahw is not required by omitting them, because they are not intended ends; rather the intended end is the recitation itself, so they become like the standing (qawma). The proof of obligation is that recitation is from the pillars of prayer, and in the obligatory prayers it is performed in a manner of publicity not concealment. For this reason, the Prophet (peace be upon him) used to recite aloud in all prayers at the beginning until the disbelievers intended not to hear the Qur'an, and they would play therein, so Allah the Exalted revealed: 'And do not recite aloud in your prayer nor quietly, but seek a way between that' (al-Isra': 110) – that he should recite aloud in the night prayer and quietly in the day prayer, because they were prepared for harm in the day prayer. And he used to recite aloud in the two 'Ids and Jumu'ah because their establishment was in Madinah with a congregation, and the disbelievers at that time did not have the power to harm. Then, even if this excuse disappeared, the ruling remains, like the raml (quick walking) in tawaf. And because he (peace be upon him) continuously practiced both throughout his life, so they are wajib. (Fath, Mustakhlas)
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling of jahr (loud recitation) and israr (quiet recitation) in prayer. Some scholars hold they are sunnah, arguing that the purpose is the recitation itself, and the manner is secondary. However, the Hanafi position, as presented here, is that they are wajib. The evidence is the Prophet's continuous practice (muwazabah) and the Quranic verse that differentiated between loud and quiet recitation based on context (night vs. day). The verse from Surah al-Isra' instructed the Prophet to recite aloud at night and quietly during the day to avoid harm from disbelievers. After that, the ruling became established and remained even when the excuse was gone, similar to the raml (quick walking) in tawaf which was originally due to showing strength but remained as a sunnah. The text argues that because the Prophet maintained this practice throughout his life, it indicates obligation (wujub). Therefore, if one deliberately changes the prescribed manner (e.g., reciting aloud in a quiet prayer), sajdat al-sahw is required.

Key Terms
الجهر (al-jahr): Reciting aloud in prayer, done in certain prayers like Fajr, Maghrib, 'Isha', Jumu'ah, and 'Id.
الإسرار (al-israr): Reciting quietly in prayer, done in Zuhr and 'Asr.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which prayers does the Hanafi school require loud recitation (jahr)?
A) Zuhr and 'Asr only.
B) Fajr, Maghrib, 'Isha', Jumu'ah, and 'Id prayers.
C) Only in the two 'Id prayers.
D) Only in the night prayers.

Key Takeaway
Jahr and israr are wajib in their designated prayers; deliberate omission requires sajdat al-sahw.

The Ruling of the Final Salutation (Salam) and the Hanafi-Shafi'i Dispute
Determines the legal status (fard/wajib) and wording of the salam in prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds the salam is wajib, not fard, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the hadith of Ibn 'Umar.

Core Idea
The salam is wajib, not fard — the prayer is complete without it in case of incapacity.

When you pray, you end with the salam. But how binding is that final turn? Is it an absolute pillar or an obligatory act that can be compensated? This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq tackles exactly that distinction, contrasting the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions. We will walk through the evidence and see how the Hanafi school uses a single hadith to classify the salam as wajib, not fard, and why the second taslim is sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position on the salam
State clearly the Hanafi ruling: the salam is wajib, consists of two utterances without 'alaykum, and the second taslim is sunnah.
2
Identify the Shafi'i position and their proof
The Shafi'i school considers the salam fard, based on the hadith 'and its tahlil is the taslim'.
3
Examine the Hanafi dalil: the hadith of Ibn 'Umar
Read the hadith: if the imam has an event after the final sitting before salam, his prayer is complete. This shows the salam is not a rukn.
4
Understand the Hanafi rebuttal
The Hanafis argue that the hadith of Ibn 'Umar overrides the general implication of the other hadith, proving the salam is wajib, not fard.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: forgetting the salam requires sajdah al-sahw in the Hanafi school, but invalidates the prayer in the Shafi'i school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولفظ السلام [مرتين دون عليكم ، وقال الشافعي: فرض (ط) للمواظبة، وأفاد أن "عليكم" ليس منه كالتحويل يميناً وشمالاً، فلو اقتدى به بعد لفظ السلام قبل قوله: "عليكم" لا يصح الاقتداء، ويخرج من بتسليمة عند عامة العلماء، وقيل بتسليمتين، وأطلق في وجوب السلام فعم التسليمتين وهو الأصح، وقيل: الثانية سنة، وعند الشافعي: لفظ السلام فرض؛ لقوله عليه وتحليلها التسليم ، ولنا ما روي عن ابن عمر قال: قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: "إذا قعد الإمام في آخر صلاته ثم أحدث قبل أن يسلم وفي رواية "قبل أن يتكلم تمت صلاته"، رواه أبو داود والترمذي، وما رواه لا يفيد إلا الوجوب، وقد قلنا به.
And the wording of the salutation is twice without 'alaykum, and al-Shafi'i said: it is obligatory due to continuous practice, and it conveyed that 'alaykum is not part of it, like turning right and left. So if someone follows him after the word of salam before his saying 'alaykum, the following is not valid. And one leaves [prayer] with one taslim according to most scholars, and it is said with two taslims. He spoke generally about the obligation of salam, so it includes both taslims, and that is the correct view; it is said the second is sunnah. According to al-Shafi'i: the word of salam is obligatory because of his saying, peace be upon him, 'and its tahlil is the taslim.' Our proof is what is narrated from Ibn 'Umar: he said, the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, said: 'When the imam sits in the final part of his prayer, then he has had an event before he salutes' and in another narration 'before he speaks, his prayer is complete.' Reported by Abu Dawud and al-Tirmidhi. What he narrated does not indicate except obligation, and we have said it.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage discusses the final salutation (salam) in prayer, a ruling within the Hanafi school. The author states that the salam consists of two utterances, without the word 'alaykum (upon you). Al-Shafi'i holds that the salam is obligatory (fard), but the Hanafis argue it is wajib, not fard, based on a hadith of Ibn 'Umar. The hadith shows that if the imam has an event (nullifies wudu) after the final sitting but before speaking, the prayer is complete, implying the salam is not an absolute integral but a wajib. The Hanafis also note that exiting prayer with one taslim is acceptable, but two are recommended.

Key Terms
السلام (al-salam): The final salutation in prayer, consisting of the words 'al-salamu 'alaykum wa rahmatullah' (twice or once).
الوجوب (al-wujub): The legal obligation of an act that is binding but not as strong as fard; omission requires compensation but does not invalidate the act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling of the final salutation (salam) in prayer?
A) Fard (obligatory integral)
B) Sunnah (recommended)
C) Wajib (obligatory but not fard)
D) Mubah (permissible)

Key Takeaway
The salam in prayer is wajib (not fard) in the Hanafi school, and the second taslim is sunnah; omitting it does not invalidate the prayer but requires sajdah al-sahw if forgotten.

The Qunut of Witr: Hanafi vs Shafi'i Positions
Defines the qunut of witr and determines its ruling and nature

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on qunut of witr, explain the Hanafi proof, and contrast it with the Shafi'i position, identifying the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence).

Core Idea
Qunut of witr is a general supplication that is wajib year-round; the specific du'a is sunnah.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq defines the qunut of witr prayer and clarifies its ruling. It addresses an inter-madhhab dispute: the Hanafi school holds that qunut in witr is wajib (obligatory) every night of the year, while Imam al-Shafi'i restricts it to the latter half of Ramadan and also considers qunut in Fajr obligatory. By examining the proofs each school presents, we will understand the Hanafi tarjih and its practical impact on daily worship.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position and its elements
The Hanafi school rules that qunut of witr is wajib, meaning any supplication (not just the famous du'a) suffices. The specific du'a 'Allahumma inna nasta'inuka' is sunnah. The takbir before qunut is also wajib. This applies every night of the year, including Ramadan.
Qunut is performed after recitation in the third rak'ah.
2
Identify the Shafi'i position and its basis
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that qunut in witr is only performed in the latter half of Ramadan, based on 'Umar's instruction to Ubayy ibn Ka'b. He also considers qunut in Fajr prayer as wajib (or sunnah mu'akkadah) because the Prophet performed it after ruku' in Fajr.
Shafi'is recite qunut in Fajr daily, but witr qunut is limited to Ramadan.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response regarding witr qunut
The Hanafi proof is that the Prophet commanded qunut in witr without specifying any month. The command is general, so it applies year-round. 'Umar's instruction to Ubayy was his own ijtihad, not a binding text from the Prophet (peace be upon him).
The wording 'min ghayr fasl' means the Prophet did not separate or restrict the command.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response regarding Fajr qunut
The Hanafis argue that the Prophet performed qunut in Fajr for only one month, supplicating against the tribes of Ra'l and Dhakwan, and then abandoned it. This shows it was a temporary measure, not an established sunnah, let alone wajib. Had it been wajib, he would not have abandoned it.
This is supported by Sahih al-Bukhari and Sahih Muslim.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The difference affects daily practice: Hanafis recite qunut in witr every night of the year and do not recite qunut in Fajr. Shafi'is recite qunut in witr only in the latter half of Ramadan and recite qunut in Fajr daily. This shapes the worship routines of millions of Muslims.
Both positions are legitimate scholarly opinions (ikhtilaf).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقنوت الوتر: [هو مطلق الدعاء سواء كان في رمضان أو في غيره] أي الدعاء الواقع في صلاة الوتر، فالإضافة لأدنى ملابسة والقنوت مطلق الدعاء، وأما خصوص: "اللهم إنا نستعينك" إلى آخره فسنة، حتى لو أتى بغيره جاز إجماعاً، وكذا تكبيرة القنوت بعد فراغه من قراءة الركعة الثالثة واجبة وعند الشافعي: في النصف الأخير من رمضان؛ لأن عمر له أمر به أبي بن كعب في النصف منه، ولنا: ما ورد أنه عليه أمر به في الوتر من غير فصل، والمراد بالقنوت فيما رواه طول القراءة، وأيضاً القنوت عنده في الصبح واجب؛ لأنه عليه قنت في الفجر بعد الركوع، ولنا: أنه عليه قنت شهراً يدعو على رعل وذكوان ثم تركه، رواه البخاري ومسلم.
And the qunut of witr: it is absolute supplication, whether in Ramadan or other than it, i.e., the supplication occurring in witr prayer. Thus the idafah (construct) is for minimal correlation, and qunut is absolute supplication. As for the specific: 'O Allah, we seek Your help' etc., it is sunnah, so much so that if one comes with other than it, it is permissible by consensus. Likewise, the takbir of qunut after finishing the recitation of the third rak'ah is wajib. According to al-Shafi'i: in the latter half of Ramadan, because 'Umar commanded Ubayy ibn Ka'b to do it in the latter half. Our proof: what is reported that the Prophet, peace be upon him, commanded it in witr without specification. What is meant by qunut in what he narrated is the length of recitation. Also, al-Shafi'i considers qunut in Fajr obligatory because the Prophet performed qunut in Fajr after bowing. Our proof: that the Prophet performed qunut for a month, supplicating against the tribes of Ra'l and Dhakwan, then abandoned it, reported by al-Bukhari and Muslim.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage defines the qunut of witr prayer and its ruling. The author states that the qunut is wajib (obligatory) and is any supplication, while the specific well-known du'a ('Allahumma inna nasta'inuka') is sunnah. The takbir before qunut is also wajib. Al-Shafi'i restricts qunut to the latter half of Ramadan and also considers qunut in Fajr obligatory. The Hanafi proof is that the Prophet commanded qunut in witr without specifying a time, and the practice of 'Umar with Ubayy was based on the Prophet's command. Regarding qunut in Fajr, the Prophet did it only for a month and then abandoned it, showing it is not established as wajib. Thus, the Hanafi school holds qunut of witr as wajib year-round, while qunut in Fajr is not.

Key Terms
القنوت (al-qunut): A supplication recited in prayer, specifically in witr, after the recitation and bowing, begging Allah's help and guidance.
الوتر (al-witr): The witr prayer, an odd-numbered prayer performed after the 'isha' prayer, consisting of three rak'ahs in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Wajib (obligatory) and performed year-round
C) Fard (obligatory integral)
D) Mubah (optional)

Key Takeaway
The qunut of witr is wajib year-round in the Hanafi school; the specific du'a is sunnah and any supplication fulfills the obligation.

Ruling on Takbirs of the Two Eids
Determines obligation of extra takbirs in Eid prayers via istihsan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds the extra takbirs in Eid prayers as wajib and justify it using istihsan over qiyas.

Core Idea
The extra takbirs of Eid are wajib because they are attributed to the entire prayer, not just parts.

In this passage, the author establishes the ruling of the extra takbirs in Eid prayers. The default qiyas would classify them as sunnah, but the Hanafi school employs istihsan to elevate them to wajib. The key distinction is that these takbirs are specifically associated with the entire prayer, unlike other dhikr that are confined to particular postures.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the extra takbirs in the two Eid prayers. According to the Hanafi school, they are wajib (obligatory).
The ruling is not fard because the evidence is zanni in dalalah, but it is still binding.
2
Locate the primary dalil
The author cites Quran 2:185 (وَلِتُكْمِلُوا الْعِدَّةَ وَلِتُكَبِّرُوا اللهَ) which commands takbir after completing the fast. This is interpreted as referring to Eid al-Fitr takbirs and extended by istihsan to both Eids.
The verse is qati' al-thubut but zanni al-dalalah regarding obligation.
3
Understand the qiyas argument
Some scholars, including a minority within the Hanafi school, argue that the takbirs are sunnah by qiyas, comparing them to other dhikr like ta'awwudh, because prayer is based on actions, not dhikr, and the Prophet never performed sajdah al-sahw for omitting dhikr.
This is the default analogy that would apply if not for istihsan.
4
Understand the istihsan argument
The Hanafi school prefers istihsan: these takbirs are attributed to the entire prayer (e.g., 'takbirs of the two Eids') similar to tashahhud and qunut, making them specific characteristics of the prayer, unlike tasbihat which are only for bowing and prostration. Thus, omitting them requires sajdah al-sahw.
Istihsan overrides qiyas because the attribute of 'entire prayer' is a stronger indicator.
5
Conclude the mufta bihi and consequence
The relied-upon position is wajib. If a person forgets the extra takbirs, the prayer is still valid but sajdah al-sahw is required.
This is the standard Hanafi fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتكبيرات العيدين: أي الواقعة في الصلاة، وبعدها أو الواقعة في الصلاة كما في عيد الفطر، ووجوبها لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَلِتُكْمِلُوا الْعِدَّةَ وَلِتُكَبِّرُوا اللهَ عَلَى مَا هَدَاكُمْ﴾ (البقرة: 185)، وقيل: المراد منه تكبيرات العيدين كما يفهم، وقيل: قنوت الوتر وتكبيرات العيدين سنة، وهو القياس؛ لأنها من الأذكار كالتعوذ والثناء؛ لأن من "البدائع"، الصلاة على الأفعال دون الأذكار، ولم ينقل أيضاً أنه عليه سجد للسهو إلا في الأفعال، ووجه الاستحسان: أن هذه الأذكار تضاف إلى جميع الصلاة، يقال: تشهد الصلاة وقنوت الوتر وتكبيرات العيدين، فصارت من خصائصها بخلاف التسبيحات فإنها تضاف للركوع والسجود فقط، فلا يجب السجدة لتركها.
And the takbirs of the two Eids: i.e., those occurring in the prayer, and after it, or those occurring in the prayer as in Eid al-Fitr. Their obligation is due to His statement: '{And to complete the number and to magnify Allah for having guided you}' (al-Baqarah 2:185). It is said: what is intended by it is the takbirs of the two Eids, as understood. And it is said: qunut of witr and takbirs of the two Eids are sunnah, and that is qiyas, because they are dhikr like ta'awwudh and thana'; because from al-Bada'i', prayer is based on actions, not dhikr. It is also not reported that the Prophet, peace be upon him, performed sajdah al-sahw except for actions. The basis of istihsan: these dhikr are attributed to the entire prayer: one says 'tashahhud of the prayer, qunut of witr, and takbirs of the two Eids.' Thus they become among its specific characteristics, unlike the tasbihat, which are attributed only to bowing and prostration. So sajdah is not obligated for leaving them.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling of the extra takbirs in Eid prayers. The author states that these takbirs are wajib (obligatory) based on the Quranic verse commanding takbir after completing the fast. Some opine they are sunnah, which is the qiyas (analogy) because they are mere dhikr, and the Prophet only performed sajdah al-sahw for actions, not dhikr. However, the istihsan (juristic preference) is that these takbirs are wajib because they are specific to the entire prayer and attributed to it, like tashahhud and qunut. This makes them more integral than other dhikr. The Hanafi school follows istihsan in this matter.

Key Terms
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): A method of juristic preference where a stronger evidence or consideration overrides a qiyas (analogy).
تكبيرات العيدين (takbirat al-'idayn): The extra utterances of 'Allahu Akbar' in the Eid prayers, performed in the first rak'ah before recitation (three times) and in the second after recitation (three times).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) A hadith that explicitly commands them
B) The Quranic verse 'li-tukabbiru' combined with istihsan
C) Consensus of all scholars
D) Qiyas (analogy) to tashahhud

Key Takeaway
The extra takbirs of Eid are wajib by istihsan because they are attributed to the entire prayer, unlike other dhikr.

Raising Hands at the Opening Takbir
Ruling statement with proof (hadith and analysis)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on raising hands at the opening takbir, explain its sunnah nature, and describe the proper manner and place.

Core Idea
Raising hands at the opening takbir is sunnah mu'akkadah, proven by a hadith listing it among seven occasions.

The first sunnah of prayer is raising the hands at the opening takbir. This practice is established by a hadith from Ibn 'Abbas and Ibn 'Umar and is considered a confirmed sunnah. The scholars discuss four aspects: the principle, the time, the manner, and the place.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that raising hands at the opening takbir is a sunnah (recommended) act. Note that it is not obligatory; the prayer is valid without it.
The opening takbir (al-tahrimah) is a rukn; raising hands is separate and sunnah.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the hadith cited: 'Hands are not raised except in seven places,' which includes the opening takbir. Understand that this is a hadith ahad, so it establishes sunnah, not obligation.
The hadith is narrated by Ibn 'Abbas and Ibn 'Umar.
3
Analyze the four sub-aspects
Break down the sunnah into: a) the principle (it is sunnah), b) time (simultaneous with takbir), c) manner (fingers spread, open hands), d) place (hands to ears, thumbs near earlobes).
These details are derived from practice and narration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسننها: رفع اليدين للتحريمة ونشر أصابعه وجهر الإمام بالتكبير، والثناء والتعوذ والتسمية والتأمين سراً. (وسننها: أي سنن الصلاة ثلاثة وعشرون الأول: رفع اليدين (ع) للتحريمة : أي تركها بحالها بلا ضم ولا تفريج. أي من السنن رفع اليدين في التكبيرة الأولى والكلام فيه في أربعة مواضع في أصل الرفع، وفي وقته، وفي كيفيته، وفي محله. أما أصل الرفع فلما روي عن ابن عباس وابن عمر عن النبي عليه أنه قال: "لا ترفع الأيدي إلا في سبع مواطن"، وعد من جملتها تكبيرة الافتتاح، وأما وقته فوقت التكبير يكون مقارناً لها؛ لأن سنة التكبير شرع لإعلام الأصم بالشروع في الصلاة، ولا يحصل هذا المقصود إلا بالمقارنة، وأما كيفيته فيرفع يديه مفتوحتين لا مضمومتين حتى يكون الأصابع نحو القبلة ويتركها بحالها، أما محله فيرفع يديه حذاء أذنيه أي يحاذي بإلهاميه شحمتي أذنيه، وكذلك في كل موضع يرفع الأيدي عند التكبير. (مستخلص) وجهر الإمام أي بقدر الحاجة وأما التبليغ عند عدم الحاجة إليه بأن يبلغ المقتدي للناس صوت الإمام فمكروه، واتفق الأئمة الأربعة على أن التبليغ في هذه الحالة بدعة مكروهة، وأما عند الاحتياج إليه فمستحب، والمنفرد والمقتدي يخفي التكبير ؛ لأن الأصل في الأذكار الإخفاء، والحاجة معدومة في حقهما بخلاف الإمام؛ فإن له حاجة. (فتح، مستخلص) بالتكبير وكذا بالتسميع والسلام لحاجته إلى الإعلام بالشروع والانتقال والمؤتم نفسه.
And among its sunan (recommended acts) are: raising the hands for the opening takbir, spreading his fingers, the imam reciting the takbir aloud, and the thana', ta'awwudh, tasmiyah, and ta'min silently. (And among its sunan: meaning the sunan of prayer are twenty-three. The first: raising the hands for the opening takbir: i.e., leaving them as they are, without closing or spreading. Raising the hands in the first takbir is from the sunan. The discussion concerns four points: the principle of raising, its time, its manner, and its place. As for the principle of raising, based on what was narrated from Ibn 'Abbas and Ibn 'Umar from the Prophet, peace be upon him, that he said: 'Hands are not raised except in seven places,' and among them he counted the opening takbir. As for its time, it is contemporaneous with the takbir, because the sunnah of takbir was legislated to inform the deaf [and others] of the commencement of prayer, and this purpose is only achieved by simultaneity. As for its manner, he raises his hands open, not closed, so that the fingers are towards the qiblah and leaves them as they are. As for its place, he raises his hands to the level of his ears, i.e., he aligns his thumbs with the earlobes. And similarly in every place where hands are raised at the takbir. And the imam's raising [of his voice] in the takbir is to the extent of need. As for transmitting (tabligh) when there is no need, such that the follower transmits the imam's voice to the people, it is makruh. The four imams agreed that tabligh in this situation is a disliked innovation (bid'ah makruhah). As for when there is need, it is mustahabb. The individual and the follower recite takbir quietly, because the principle regarding dhikr is silence, and the need is absent in their case, unlike the imam, who has a need. Regarding the takbir, and similarly for tasmi' and salam, because of his need to inform of the commencement of prayer and transitions, and the follower himself [informs himself by hearing].
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage enumerates the first sunnah acts of prayer: raising the hands at the opening takbir, spreading the fingers, and the imam reciting takbir loudly. The author, commenting on al-Hidayah, explains that raising hands is from the sunan of prayer. Four aspects are covered: the principle (it is sunnah based on a hadith from Ibn 'Abbas and Ibn 'Umar listing seven places, including the opening takbir), the time (simultaneous with the takbir), the manner (hands open, fingers towards qiblah), and the place (level with ears, thumbs aligning with earlobes). Then the ruling of the imam's loud recitation of takbir is discussed: it is sunnah, and it should be at a volume sufficient for those behind to hear. The 'tabligh' (transmitting the imam's voice) is makruh if there is no need; all four imams agree it is a disliked innovation. If needed, it is mustahabb. The individual and follower recite silently, as the default for dhikr is silence.

Key Terms
التحريمة (al-tahrimah): The opening takbir of the prayer that makes what was previously permissible (e.g., speech, worldly actions) forbidden (haram) during prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling of raising hands at the opening takbir?
A) Fard (obligatory)
B) Wajib (necessary)
C) Sunnah mu'akkadah (confirmed recommended)
D) Mubah (permissible)

Key Takeaway
Raising hands at the opening takbir is a confirmed sunnah, supported by a hadith and detailed by the Hanafi scholars in terms of time, manner, and place.

7 min
Imam's Loud Takbir and the Ruling of Tabligh
Ruling statement with conditional ruling on tabligh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling of the imam reciting takbir aloud, distinguish it from tabligh, and apply the conditions under which tabligh becomes permissible.

Core Idea
The imam recites takbir aloud to inform; tabligh (relaying) is a disliked innovation when unnecessary, but allowed when needed.

The second sunnah in the passage is the imam reciting the takbir aloud. This is to inform the congregation of the start and transitions of prayer. However, if a follower voluntarily relays the imam’s voice to those further back when not needed, it is considered makruh. All four imams agree on this.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
Recognize that the imam's jahr (loud recitation) in the takbir is sunnah mu'akkadah. It is not obligatory; the prayer is valid if done silently.
The imam's jahr is to serve the need of informing the congregation.
2
Understand the concept of tabligh
Tabligh is when a follower repeats the imam's words to pass them to others. Differentiate it from the imam's own jahr.
Tabligh is a separate action by the follower.
3
Apply the ruling based on need
If the congregation can hear the imam directly, tabligh is makruh (disliked). If they cannot hear, tabligh becomes mustahabb (recommended).
The default for dhikr is silence, but need overrides it.

(وسننها: أي سنن الصلاة ثلاثة وعشرون الأول: رفع اليدين (ع) للتحريمة : أي تركها بحالها بلا ضم ولا تفريج. أي من السنن رفع اليدين في التكبيرة الأولى والكلام فيه في أربعة مواضع في أصل الرفع، وفي وقته، وفي كيفيته، وفي محله. أما أصل الرفع فلما روي عن ابن عباس وابن عمر عن النبي عليه أنه قال: "لا ترفع الأيدي إلا في سبع مواطن"، وعد من جملتها تكبيرة الافتتاح، وأما وقته فوقت التكبير يكون مقارناً لها؛ لأن سنة التكبير شرع لإعلام الأصم بالشروع في الصلاة، ولا يحصل هذا المقصود إلا بالمقارنة، وأما كيفيته فيرفع يديه مفتوحتين لا مضمومتين حتى يكون الأصابع نحو القبلة ويتركها بحالها، أما محله فيرفع يديه حذاء أذنيه أي يحاذي بإلهاميه شحمتي أذنيه، وكذلك في كل موضع يرفع الأيدي عند التكبير. (فتح، مستخلص) بالتكبير وكذا بالتسميع والسلام لحاجته إلى الإعلام بالشروع والانتقال والمؤتم نفسه.
And among its sunan (recommended acts) are: raising the hands for the opening takbir, spreading his fingers, the imam reciting the takbir aloud, and the thana', ta'awwudh, tasmiyah, and ta'min silently. (And among its sunan: meaning the sunan of prayer are twenty-three. The first: raising the hands for the opening takbir: i.e., leaving them as they are, without closing or spreading. Raising the hands in the first takbir is from the sunan. The discussion concerns four points: the principle of raising, its time, its manner, and its place. As for the principle of raising, based on what was narrated from Ibn 'Abbas and Ibn 'Umar from the Prophet, peace be upon him, that he said: 'Hands are not raised except in seven places,' and among them he counted the opening takbir. As for its time, it is contemporaneous with the takbir, because the sunnah of takbir was legislated to inform the deaf [and others] of the commencement of prayer, and this purpose is only achieved by simultaneity. As for its manner, he raises his hands open, not closed, so that the fingers are towards the qiblah and leaves them as they are. As for its place, he raises his hands to the level of his ears, i.e., he aligns his thumbs with the earlobes. And similarly in every place where hands are raised at the takbir. And the imam's raising [of his voice] in the takbir is to the extent of need. As for transmitting (tabligh) when there is no need, such that the follower transmits the imam's voice to the people, it is makruh. The four imams agreed that tabligh in this situation is a disliked innovation (bid'ah makruhah). As for when there is need, it is mustahabb. The individual and the follower recite takbir quietly, because the principle regarding dhikr is silence, and the need is absent in their case, unlike the imam, who has a need. Regarding the takbir, and similarly for tasmi' and salam, because of his need to inform of the commencement of prayer and transitions, and the follower himself [informs himself by hearing].
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage enumerates the first sunnah acts of prayer: raising the hands at the opening takbir, spreading the fingers, and the imam reciting takbir loudly. The author, commenting on al-Hidayah, explains that raising hands is from the sunan of prayer. Four aspects are covered: the principle (it is sunnah based on a hadith from Ibn 'Abbas and Ibn 'Umar listing seven places, including the opening takbir), the time (simultaneous with the takbir), the manner (hands open, fingers towards qiblah), and the place (level with ears, thumbs aligning with earlobes). Then the ruling of the imam's loud recitation of takbir is discussed: it is sunnah, and it should be at a volume sufficient for those behind to hear. The 'tabligh' (transmitting the imam's voice) is makruh if there is no need; all four imams agree it is a disliked innovation. If needed, it is mustahabb. The individual and follower recite silently, as the default for dhikr is silence.

Key Terms
التبليغ (al-tabligh): Acting as a relay for the imam's voice when the congregation cannot hear him directly, e.g., by having one follower repeat the takbir loudly.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Mustahabb (recommended)

C) Wajib (obligatory)
D) Mubah (permissible)

Key Takeaway
The imam's loud takbir is a sunnah; tabligh by a follower is disliked if unnecessary, but recommended when needed to inform those who cannot hear.

Silent Dhikr in Prayer: Thana', Ta'awwudh, Tasmiyah, and Ta'min
Determines the rulings of thana', ta'awwudh, tasmiyah, and ta'min in prayer, including disagreements

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on thana', ta'awwudh, tasmiyah, and ta'min, explain why each is recited silently, and identify the opposing positions of the Shafi'i and Maliki schools.

Core Idea
All four silent dhikr in prayer are sunnah and recited silently; the Hanafi position is grounded in hadith and the principle of ikhfa'.

The opening segment of prayer involves several utterances beyond the takbir and al-Fatihah. The Hanafi school holds that thana' (praise), ta'awwudh (seeking refuge), tasmiyah (Bismillah), and ta'min (Amin) are all sunnah and must be recited silently. This lesson unpacks the evidence for these rulings and the disagreements with Imam al-Shafi'i and Imam Malik, enabling you to distinguish the operative Hanafi position from alternative views.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on each dhikr
Identify the ruling for thana', ta'awwudh, tasmiyah, and ta'min according to the Hanafi school: all are sunnah and recited silently.
Note that thana' and ta'awwudh are silent by agreement; the dispute is over tasmiyah and ta'min.
2
Identify the opposing schools and their views
Recognize that Imam al-Shafi'i considers tasmiyah fard (obligatory) and recited aloud, and ta'min recited aloud. Imam Malik does not consider thana' and ta'awwudh as sunnah at all, and he omits tasmiyah.
Malik's view on tasmiyah is that it is not recited; Shafi'i recites it aloud as part of al-Fatihah.
3
Examine the proofs of each side
For the Hanafis: the hadith of Anas that the Prophet did not recite Bismillah aloud, and the hadith of Wa'il ibn Hujr for silent Amin. For Shafi'i: a report of the Prophet reciting Bismillah aloud and the hadith of Abu Hurayrah implying audible Amin.
Focus on the specific wording of the hadith and how each school derives its ruling.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to opponent's proofs
The Hanafis interpret the Shafi'i proof for tasmiyah as a teaching instance, not the norm, and argue that the hadith of Abu Hurayrah for ta'min is answered by the hadith of Wa'il which shows the imam's Amin is silent.
The principle that dhikr is originally concealed (ikhfa') also supports the Hanafi view.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The practical difference: Shafi'is recite tasmiyah and ta'min aloud; Hanafis recite them silently. Shafi'is consider tasmiyah fard; Hanafis consider it sunnah.
In a mixed congregation, this difference may cause confusion, but each school follows its own established practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثناء وهو قراءة سبحانك اللهم إلخ، وعند مالك: ليس بسنة. [رمز الحقائق: ٥٤/١] والتعوّذ: أي قراءة: أعوذ بالله من الشيطان الرجيم، وعند مالك: ليس بسنة. (ع) التسمية: يعني قراءة: بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم وعند الشافعي هي فرض؛ لأنها من الفاتحة، وعند مالك: لا يقرأها بل يبدأ بالفاتحة. (ع) التأمين: وهو أن يقول بعد الفاتحة آمين وهي سنة في حق الإمام والمأموم. سراً: يتعلق بالكل من الثناء والتعوذ والتسمية والتأمين أي يأتي بهذه الأربعة سراً، أما الثناء والتعوذ فيأتي بهما سراً بالاتفاق، وأما التسمية والتأمين فيجهر بهما عند الشافعي، أما التسمية فلما أن روي النبي عليه جهر في صلاته بالتسمية، قلنا: هو محمول على تعليم أنها بين التعوذ والقراءة؛ لأن أنسا أخبر أن النبي عليه كان لا يجهر بها، وحجته في التأمين حديث أبي هريرة عن النبي عليه أنه قال: "إذا أمن الإمام فأمنوا، فإن الملائكة يؤمنون، فمن وافق تأمينه تأمين الملائكة غفر الله له ما تقدم من ذنبه وما تأخر"، فلو لم يكن تأمين الإمام مسموعاً لم يكن معلوماً، فلا معنى للتعليق، ولنا ما روي عن وائل بن حجر أن النبي عليه قال: "إذا قال الإمام: "ولا الضالين" فقولوا: "آمين"؛ فإن الإمام يقولها والملائكة يقولون بها، فإن كان مسموعاً لما احتيج إلى قوله: "فإن الإمام يقولها"، وأيضاً هو من الأذكار، والأصل فيها الإخفاء.
And thana' (praise) which is reciting 'Subhanaka Allahumma' etc. According to Malik, it is not sunnah. And ta'awwudh (seeking refuge): i.e., reciting 'A'udhu billahi min al-shaytan al-rajim.' According to Malik, it is not sunnah. And tasmiyah: i.e., reciting 'Bismillah al-Rahman al-Rahim.' According to al-Shafi'i, it is obligatory (fard) because it is part of Surah al-Fatihah. According to Malik, he does not recite it but begins with al-Fatihah. And ta'min (saying Amin): which is to say after al-Fatihah 'Amin.' It is sunnah for both the imam and the follower. 'Silently' relates to all four (thana', ta'awwudh, tasmiyah, ta'min): i.e., they are recited silently. As for thana' and ta'awwudh, they are recited silently by agreement. As for tasmiyah and ta'min, al-Shafi'i recites them aloud. Regarding tasmiyah, it is reported that the Prophet recited it aloud in his prayer. We say: that is interpreted as teaching that it is between ta'awwudh and recitation, because Anas informed that the Prophet did not recite it aloud. His evidence for ta'min is the hadith of Abu Hurayrah from the Prophet: 'When the imam says Amin, then say Amin, for the angels say Amin. Whoever's Amin coincides with the angels' Amin, his past and future sins will be forgiven.' If the imam's Amin was not audible, it would not be known, so the conditioning would be meaningless. Our proof is what is reported from Wa'il ibn Hujr that the Prophet said: 'When the imam says [wa la] al-dallin, then say Amin, for the imam says it and the angels say it.' If it were audible, there would be no need for him to say 'for the imam says it.' Also, it is from the dhikr, and the origin regarding them is concealment.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage discusses four silent dhikr in the initial part of the prayer: thana' (praising Allah), ta'awwudh (seeking refuge), tasmiyah (Bismillah), and ta'min (Amin). The author presents the Hanafi position that all four are sunnah and should be recited silently. Disagreements from other schools are noted: Imam Malik considers thana' and ta'awwudh as not sunnah; Imam al-Shafi'i considers tasmiyah as fard (obligatory) because it is a verse of al-Fatihah, and he also recites ta'min aloud. The Hanafi proofs are clear: for tasmiyah, the claim that the Prophet recited it aloud is interpreted as a teaching instance, as Anas reports he did not do so regularly. For ta'min, the hadith of Abu Hurayrah would imply the imam's Amin is audible if the congregation must hear it, but the hadith of Wa'il ibn Hujr shows that the Prophet instructed the congregation to say Amin without relying on hearing the imam, indicating it is recited silently. Moreover, the principle for all dhikr is silence (ikhfa').

Key Terms
الثناء (al-thana'): The supplication of praise recited at the beginning of prayer after the opening takbir, such as 'Subhanaka Allahumma'. In Hanafi, it is sunnah and recited silently.
التعوذ (al-ta'awwudh): Seeking refuge in Allah from the accursed devil, recited after thana' before Bismillah. Hanafi: sunnah and silent.
التسمية (al-tasmiyah): Reciting 'Bismillah al-Rahman al-Rahim' before al-Fatihah. Hanafi: sunnah and silent, and not a verse of al-Fatihah.
التأمين (al-ta'min): Saying 'Amin' after reciting al-Fatihah. In Hanafi: sunnah for both imam and follower, recited silently.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Silently, and it is sunnah
C) Not recited at all
D) Only on Fridays

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school maintains that thana', ta'awwudh, tasmiyah, and ta'min are all sunnah and recited silently, in contrast to Shafi'i and Maliki views.

Sunnah of Jahr and Sirr in Prayer
Explains which parts of prayer are said aloud or silently

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on which parts of the prayer the imam recites aloud or silently, and explain the evidence from hadith for the silent recitation of tasmiyah and ameen.

Core Idea
The imam recites takbir, tasmi', and salam aloud; thana', ta'awwudh, tasmiyah, and ameen are sunnah recited silently.

In this lesson, we examine a passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq that details which invocations in prayer are performed audibly by the imam and which are silent. The text also engages with the views of Imam Malik and Imam al-Shafi'i, providing Hanafi proofs for the silent recitation of tasmiyah and ameen. Understanding these distinctions is essential for proper congregational prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the imam's audible invocations
The text lists takbir, tasmi' (sami'a Allahu liman hamidah), and salam as recited aloud to inform the congregation about transitions.
The follower does not say these aloud.
2
Identify the silent sunnah invocations
Thana' (Subhanaka), ta'awwudh (A'udhu billah), tasmiyah (Bismillah), and ameen are all sunnah and performed silently by the imam.
Malik does not consider thana' and ta'awwudh as sunnah; Shafi'i considers tasmiyah fard and ameen aloud.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proofs for silent tasmiyah
The hadith of Anas that the Prophet did not recite tasmiyah aloud is used; the hadith of loud recitation is interpreted as teaching placement.
This shows the Hanafi method of reconciling hadith.
4
Analyze the Hanafi proofs for silent ameen
The hadith of Wa'il ibn Hujr: when the imam says 'nor those who go astray,' say 'ameen' for the imam says it. If it were audible, the instruction would be redundant.
This is the key Hanafi proof against the Shafi'i view.
5
Conclude the relied-upon Hanafi practice
The mufta bihi is that thana', ta'awwudh, tasmiyah, and ameen are all recited silently by the imam.
This is the practice in Hanafi mosques worldwide.

And the imam's raising his voice in takbir, and likewise in tasmi' (sami'a Allahu liman hamidah) and salam, due to his need to inform of starting and transitioning; and the follower himself [does not raise his voice]. And the thana' (praise) which is reciting 'Subhanaka Allahumma...' etc., and according to Malik it is not sunnah. Commentary
This passage covers several sunan: imam's jahr in takbir, tasmi', and salam; thana', ta'awwudh, tasmiyah, and ameen are all to be recited silently. It includes disputes with Malik and Shafi'i. For thana' and ta'awwudh, Malik does not consider them sunnah. For tasmiyah, Shafi'i considers it fard; Hanafis consider it sunnah and say it silently. For ameen, Shafi'i says imam says it aloud; Hanafis say silently, with proofs from hadith.

Key Terms
الجهر (al-jahr): Raising the voice enough to be heard by those near.
السر (as-sirr): Reciting silently, moving the lips.
التسمية (at-tasmiyah): Reciting 'Bismillah ir-Rahman ir-Rahim' before Fatiha.
التأمين (at-ta'meen): Saying 'Ameen' after Fatiha.

Key Takeaway

Hand Placement in Prayer: Navel vs Chest
Ruling with proof and inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on hand placement for men in prayer, cite the hadith proofs, and explain the difference with the Shafi'i school.

Core Idea
Hands under navel in prayer is sunnah muakkadah based on hadith of Ali and Wa'il.

This passage addresses a well-known dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the placement of hands during the standing posture in prayer. The Hanafi school holds that the right hand should be placed on the left under the navel, while the Shafi'i school places them on the chest. We will examine the textual proofs and reasoning for each position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi ruling
The Hanafi school rules that a man places his right hand on his left under the navel during qiyam. This is a sunnah muakkadah (emphasized sunnah).
Not fard or wajib.
2
Identify the opposing school and their position
The Shafi'i (and Hanbali) school places the hands on the chest. Their reasoning: the Prophet placed on chest and it is closer to humility.
They also consider it closer to khushu'.
3
Examine the Hanafi proof (dalil)
Two hadith are cited: from Ali: 'It is from the sunnah to place the right on the left under the navel'; from Wa'il: 'I saw the Prophet place his right on his left under the navel'. Also a rational argument: reverence before kings.
These are ahad hadith (zanni al-thubut).
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to the opposing view
The Hanafi response emphasizes that the hadith evidence directly specifies under navel, and the analogy to kings' court supports reverence (ta'zim) as the spirit of prayer.
The text contrasts the Shafi'i emphasis on humility vs Hanafi emphasis on reverence.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The difference is the physical hand posture during qiyam. Both views are valid; the Hanafi school recommends its position as the sunnah. The prayer remains valid even if the other posture is adopted, but leaving a sunnah muakkadah is makruh tanzihan.
The passage also clarifies that placing hands over the 'awrah (above clothing) is not harmful.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ووضع يمينه على يساره تحت سرته. ووضع يمينه: وعند الشافعي رحمه الله: يضع على صدره؛ لأنه كان لا يضع على الصدر؛ ولأنه أقرب إلى الخضوع من الوضع على العورة. ولنا حديث علي بن أبي طالب رضي الله عنه: "من السنة وضع اليمين على الشمال تحت السرة"؛ ولأنه أقرب إلى التعظيم كما بين يدي الملوك. ووضعهما على العورة لا يضر فوق الثياب؛ لأنها ليس لها حكم العورة في حقه، ولهذا تضع المرأة يديها على صدرها وإن كان عورة. تبيين الحقائق: ٢٧٩/١٨. ولنا أيضاً حديث وائل بن حجر قال: رأيت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم وضع يمينه على شماله تحت السرة. (فتح) تحت سرته: وضعهما تحت سرته، وعند الشافعي وأحمد على صدره. (ع)
And placing his right hand on his left under his navel. And regarding 'placing his right hand': According to al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, he places it on his chest, because he (the Prophet) used to place on the chest, and because it is closer to humility than placing on the private part. And our proof is the hadith of Ali ibn Abi Talib, may Allah be pleased with him: 'It is from the sunnah to place the right on the left under the navel,' and because it is closer to reverence as before kings. And placing them on the private part does not harm above clothing, because the private part has no ruling of 'awrah for him in that case, and for this reason a woman places her hands on her chest even though it is 'awrah. Tabyin al-Haqa'iq: 279/18. And we also have the hadith of Wa'il ibn Hajar who said: I saw the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, place his right hand on his left under the navel. (Fath) 'Under his navel': He places them under his navel, and according to al-Shafi'i and Ahmad on his chest. ('Ayni)
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the placement of hands during standing (qiyam) in prayer. The Hanafi position is that the right hand should be placed on the left under the navel, based on two hadiths: one from Ali ibn Abi Talib stating that it is part of the sunnah, and one from Wa'il ibn Hajar directly observing the Prophet's practice. The reasoning given is that this posture is more akin to standing with reverence before a king. The Shafi'i school places the hands on the chest, arguing that it is closer to humility. The Hanafi response counters that the hadith evidence supports the navel placement. Additionally, the text clarifies that placing hands over the private area above clothing is not problematic, because a man's private parts are not considered 'awrah in that specific context (when covered). Women, however, place their hands on their chests because their entire body is 'awrah.

Key Terms
وضع اليمين على الشمال (wad' al-yamin 'ala al-shamal): Placing the right hand over the left hand during the standing posture (qiyam) in prayer.
تحت السرّة (taht al-surrah): Below the navel; the specific location for hand placement in the Hanafi school.
العورة (al-'awrah): Parts of the body that must be covered in prayer and before others.

The Sunnah of Takbir and Tasbih in Ruku'
Ruling on takbir and tasbih in ruku' with proofs and additional rulings on prolongation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that takbir in ruku' is sunnah, identify the minimum and complete form of tasbih, and recognize the ruling on prolonging ruku' for a latecomer.

Core Idea
Takbir and tasbih in ruku' are sunnah, not fard; one tasbih suffices, three is complete.

In this lesson, we examine the sunnah acts related to ruku' according to the Hanafi school. The Prophet's practice confirms that takbir is said when moving into ruku', and tasbih is recited at least once, preferably three times. We also clarify the rulings on prolonging ruku' for a latecomer, distinguishing between permissible and impermissible instances.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What are takbir al-rukū' and tasbīḥ al-rukū'? The Hanafi school classifies both as sunnah muakkadah.
2
Note the proof
The Prophet consistently performed takbir at each movement; the hadith instructs tasbih in ruku'.
The hadith is a solitary report (khabar wahid).
3
Understand minimum vs. complete
One tasbih fulfills the sunnah, but three is the complete sunnah. Decreasing from three is makruh tanzihan.
Makruh tanzihi means disliked but not sinful.
4
Contrast with prolongation
Prolonging ruku' to let a latecomer join is makruh tahriman if the latecomer is known, otherwise no harm.
Makruh tahrimi is closer to haram.
5
Apply the ruling
In practice, ensure at least one tasbih, preferably three, and avoid causing delay intentionally for a known latecomer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتكبير الركوع: لما روي أنه عليه السلام كان يكبر عند كل رفع وخفض [ رمز الحقائق: ٥٤/١] والرفع وهو مرفوع عطفا على التكبير ولا يجوز جره؛ لأنه لا تكبير عند الرفع، وإنما يأتي بالتسميع والتحميد عند الرفع أي الاستواء قائماً. (ع، ط) وتسبيحه لقوله عليه السلام: "إذا ركع أحدكم فليقل في ركوعه: سبحان ربي العظيم" ثلاثاً، وذلك أدناه أي أدنى كمال يعني أن سنية التسبيح تحصل ولو مرة، وكمالها يتوقف على الثلاث، لكنهم قالوا: يكره أن ينقص عن الثلاث كراهة تنزيهية، بخلاف إطالة الركوع أو القراءة ليدركه الجائي فإنها تحريمية إن عرفه وإلا فلا بأس. (فتح) وقال في "المستخلص": قال مالك: من ترك تسبيح الركوع بطلت صلاته لما روي أنه لما نزل قوله: ﴿فَسَبِّحْ بِاسْمِ رَبِّكَ الْعَظِيمِ﴾ (الواقعة: ٧٤) فقال النبي عليه السلام: "اجعلوها في ركوعكم"، ولنا قوله تعالى: ﴿ارْكَعُوا﴾ (الحج: ٧٧) مطلقاً عن شرط التسبيح فلا يجوز نسخ الكتاب بخبر الواحد، فقلنا بالجواز مع كون التسبيح سنة عملاً بالدليلين.
And the takbir of ruku': because it is narrated that he (peace be upon him) used to say takbir at every lowering and raising. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 54/1]. And 'al-raf' (the raising) is in the nominative case, coordinated with al-takbir, and it is not permissible to put it in the genitive; because there is no takbir at the raising, rather one says tasmi' and tahmid at the raising i.e. when standing straight. ('Ayni, Talghih). And his tasbih (in ruku') due to his (peace be upon him) statement: 'When one of you bows, let him say in his ruku': "Subhana Rabbiyal 'Azim" three times, and that is its minimum i.e. the minimum of perfection, meaning that the sunnah of tasbih is achieved even with one time, and its perfection depends on three. But they said: it is makruh tanzihan to decrease from three, unlike prolonging the ruku' or recitation to allow a latecomer to catch the rak'ah, for that is makruh tahriman if he knows the latecomer, otherwise no harm. (Fath) And it is said in al-Mustakhlas: Malik said: Whoever leaves the tasbih of ruku', his prayer is invalidated because when the verse {So glorify the name of your Lord, the Most Great} (al-Waqi'ah: 74) was revealed, the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Put it in your ruku'.' And our proof is the saying of Allah: {Bow down} (al-Hajj: 77) absolutely without the condition of tasbih, so it is not permissible to abrogate the Book with a solitary report. Therefore we say permissibility while the tasbih is sunnah, acting on both proofs.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage discusses the sunan acts of takbir and tasbih in ruku'. The Hanafi position is that saying takbir when going into ruku' is sunnah, supported by the Prophet's consistent practice. The tasbih ('Subhana Rabbiyal 'Azim') is also sunnah, with the minimum being once and the complete sunnah being three times. It is makruh tanzihan to say less than three. The text then addresses the position of Imam Malik, who considered the tasbih in ruku' obligatory (fard) based on the verse from Surah al-Waqi'ah and the Prophet's instruction to place that glorification in ruku'. The Hanafi response counters that the command to bow (from Surah al-Hajj) is absolute and does not specify tasbih; thus the obligation is only to bow. Adding an obligation through a solitary hadith (khabar wahid) is not allowed if it abrogates the absolute command of the Quran. Therefore, tasbih remains a sunnah, not fard. The Hanafi approach reconciles both proofs by making tasbih a recommended act.

Key Terms
تكبير الركوع (takbir al-rukū'): Saying 'Allahu Akbar' while moving into the bowing position.
تسبيح الركوع (tasbīḥ al-rukū'): Saying 'Subhana Rabbiyal 'Azim' in ruku', at least once, preferably three times.
الرفع منه (al-raf' minhu): Raising from ruku' to standing position, accompanied by 'Sami'a Allahu liman hamidah' and 'Rabbana lakal hamd'.
أدناه (adnāhu): The minimum of perfection; saying tasbih once ensures the sunnah is met, but three is more complete.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on saying 'Subhana Rabbiyal 'Azim' three times in ruku'?
A) Fard (obligatory)
B) Wajib
C) Sunnah muakkadah
D) Makruh

Key Takeaway
Takbir when moving to ruku' and tasbih in ruku' are sunnah acts; one recitation suffices but three is more perfect.

The Hanafi-Maliki Dispute on Tasbih in Ruku'
Inter-madhhab dispute on the obligation of tasbih in ruku'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling that tasbih in ruku' is sunnah not fard, state the Maliki evidence, and analyze the Hanafi rebuttal based on the principle that the Quran cannot be abrogated by a solitary hadith.

Core Idea
Tasbih in ruku' is sunnah: the absolute command to bow cannot be made conditional by a solitary report.

This lesson delves into the disagreement between the Hanafi and Maliki schools regarding the legal status of tasbih in ruku'. While both rely on Quranic verses and hadith, they interpret them differently. The Hanafi school maintains that the Quranic command to bow (Surah al-Hajj) is unconditional, and adding an obligation via a solitary hadith would amount to abrogation, which is impermissible. Hence, tasbih remains sunnah. We will examine each school's proof and the practical outcome.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
Is tasbih in ruku' obligatory (fard) or sunnah?
2
State the Hanafi position
Sunnah, based on the absolute command to bow in Quran 22:77.
3
State the Maliki position
Fard, based on Quran 56:74 and the Prophet's instruction to place that glorification in ruku'.
4
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal
The Maliki proof attempts to restrict the general command to bow, but a solitary hadith cannot abrogate the Quran.
This is a key usul al-fiqh principle: a khabar wahid cannot abrogate a qat'i text.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
In the Maliki school, omitting tasbih invalidates the prayer; in the Hanafi school, the prayer remains valid.
This difference has practical implications for followers of each school.

Source Text

Key Takeaway

Takbir and Tasbih in Sujud
Ruling on sunnah of takbir and tasbih in sujud

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the sunnah acts of takbir and tasbih in sujud according to Hanafi fiqh and identify the hadith evidence supporting them.

Core Idea
Takbir for sujud and tasbih three times are sunnah, not fard; the minimum is once.

This lesson examines two sunnah acts during prostration: the takbir when moving into sujud and the tasbih three times. We will see the Hanafi ruling and the hadith evidence that supports it, along with an editorial note about also mentioning the takbir for rising from sujud.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Acts and Rulings
Determine which acts in sujud are discussed: takbir (saying 'Allahu Akbar' when prostrating) and tasbih (saying 'Subhana Rabbiyal A'la'). Note their legal status according to the Hanafi school.
Both are sunnah muakkadah.
2
Locate the Dalil
Find the proof cited: the hadith 'When one of you prostrates, let him say in his sujud: Subhana Rabbiyal A'la three times.' This is a direct command from the Prophet.
The hadith is ahad, so it indicates sunnah, not obligation.
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah
Analyze how the hadith proves the ruling. The imperative 'let him say' is understood in Hanafi usul as recommending (sunnah) when there is no qarina that makes it obligatory. The minimum is once, but the complete sunnah is three times as per the hadith.
The text explicitly says 'three times' and 'that is its minimum'.
4
Note the Editorial Expansion
The author notes that if the original text had also mentioned 'and the raising from it' (i.e., takbir when rising from sujud), it would have been better, implying that the takbir for raising is also sunnah by analogy.
This shows an expansion of the ruling beyond the literal text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The tasbih is to be said three times, with the minimum being once, similar to ruku', as per the hadith. The passages do not contain a dispute with other schools, but simply present the Hanafi ruling.

Order of Placing Limbs in Sujud and the Seven Limbs Dispute
Ruling on the sunnah order and dispute on seven limbs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the order of placing knees and hands in sujud, and distinguish between the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions regarding the seven limbs.

Core Idea
Knees first, then hands in sujud; the seven limbs are sunnah not fard.

This lesson examines two interrelated disputes: the preferred order of placing limbs when going into prostration, and whether prostration on the seven limbs (face, hands, knees, feet) is obligatory or merely recommended. The Hanafi school places the knees before the hands, contrary to the Shafi'i and Maliki schools. Additionally, the Hanafi school considers that only the forehead and feet are essential for sujud, while hands and knees are sunnah. We will explore the proofs on both sides and understand why the Hanafi position is relied upon.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify that the Hanafi school holds that one should place the knees first, then the hands, and that placing hands and knees on the ground is sunnah, not fard.
2
Identify the opposing schools and their views
Recognize that Malik and al-Shafi'i hold that hands should be placed first, and that Zufar (a Hanafi scholar) and al-Shafi'i consider prostration on seven limbs to be fard.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the hadith forbidding imitating a camel in prayer and the hadith commanding prostration on seven bones, which are used by the opposing schools.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Understand how the Hanafi school reinterprets the same camel hadith (the camel places its hands first, so knees first) and interprets the command on seven bones as recommendation due to consensus on tied hands.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Note the practical consequence: in the Hanafi school, missing hand or knee contact does not invalidate sujud; in the Shafi'i school it does.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وضع يديه وفي النسخة التي اختارها صاحب "المستخلص": وضع يديه بعد ركبتيه بلفظ "بعد" عوض واو العطف، أي من جملة السنن أن يضع ركبتيه أولاً ثم يديه، وهذا عندنا، وقال مالك والشافعي: يضع يديه أولا؛ لما روي أن النبي عليه السلام نهى عن بروك الجمل في الصلاة، وهو يضع ركبتيه أولاً، ولنا: عين هذا الحديث؛ لأن الجمل يضع يديه أولا، وعن عمر رضي الله عنه وابن مسعود رضي الله عنه مثل مذهبنا. وفي باقي الشروح: "وركبتيه" بواو العطف وقال زفر والشافعي: السجود على الأعضاء السبعة فرض؛ لقوله عليه السلام: "أمرت أن أسجد على سبعة أعظم"، ولنا أن السجدة يتحقق بوضع الجبهة والقدمين، ولهذا جاز من شد يداه إلى خلفه بالإجماع، وما رواه محمول على الندب. (فتح) وركبتيه : أي على الأرض حالة السجود وهو سنة عندنا لتحقق السجود بدون وضعهما، وعند الشافعي وزفر: السجود فرض على الأعضاء السبعة وهي الوجه واليدان والركبتان والقدمان. (مسكين)
Placing his hands. And in the version chosen by the author of al-Mustakhlas: placing his hands after his knees, using the word 'after' instead of the conjunctive waw, meaning that it is part of the sunnah to place the knees first then the hands, and this is our (Hanafi) view. Malik and al-Shafi'i said: he places his hands first, because it is narrated that the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade the crouching of a camel in prayer, and the camel places its knees first. And our proof is the same hadith, because the camel places its hands first. And from 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) and Ibn Mas'ud (may Allah be pleased with him) is similar to our school. And in the remaining commentaries: 'and his knees' with the conjunctive waw. And Zufar and al-Shafi'i said: prostration upon seven limbs is fard, due to his (peace be upon him) statement: 'I have been commanded to prostrate on seven bones.' And our proof is that prostration is realized by placing the forehead and the feet, and for this reason it is permissible by consensus for one whose hands are tied behind his back (to prostrate), and what he narrated is interpreted as recommendation. (Fath) And his knees: i.e., on the ground during prostration, and it is sunnah with us because prostration is realized without placing them. And according to al-Shafi'i and Zufar: prostration upon seven limbs is fard, namely the face, the two hands, the two knees, and the two feet. (Miskin)
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage contains two interrelated rulings: (1) the preferred order of placing limbs when going into sujud, and (2) whether prostration on the seven limbs (including hands and knees) is fard or sunnah. On the first issue, the Hanafi school holds that one should place the knees first, then the hands, based on the same hadith that Malik and Shafi'i use for the opposite. The Hanafi interpretation is that the camel (which is forbidden to imitate) places its hands (forelegs) first, so the correct posture is to place knees first. This is also attributed to 'Umar and Ibn Mas'ud. On the second issue, Zufar (a Hanafi scholar) and al-Shafi'i consider prostration on seven limbs (face, hands, knees, feet) as fard. The Hanafi school disagrees, stating that the essential part of sujud is placing the forehead and the feet; hands and knees are sunnah. Evidence: it is permissible by consensus for a person with hands tied behind his back to prostrate, proving hands are not fard. The hadith about seven bones is interpreted as recommendation (nadb).

Key Terms
بروك الجمل (burūk al-jamal): The manner in which a camel kneels down, placing its forelegs (hands) first.
الأعضاء السبعة (al-a'ḍā' al-sab'ah): The seven body parts on which prostration is performed: face (forehead), two hands, two knees, and two feet.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) All seven
B) Four (face, hands, knees, feet)
C) Two (forehead and feet)
D) None, all are sunnah

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the sunnah order is knees first then hands, and the obligatory parts of sujud are only the forehead and feet; hands and knees are sunnah.

Sitting Posture in Prayer: Ifirash vs Tawarruk
Ruling on sunnah sitting posture and dispute on tawarruk

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on sitting posture in prayer and explain the school's response to opposing evidence.

Core Idea
Ifirash (sitting on left foot) is sunnah in all sittings; tawarruk is not sunnah and was only adopted by the Prophet due to old age.

This passage addresses a subtle but practical difference between the schools: the correct sitting posture during prayer. While many Muslims around the world sit differently, the Hanafi school has specific evidence that shapes its practice. Understanding this dispute reveals how the Hanafis reconcile conflicting hadiths.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: ifirash (sitting on left foot, right foot upright) is sunnah in all sittings, including between prostrations and both tashahhuds.
Note that the passage says 'مطلقاً' meaning absolutely (in all sittings).
2
Identify the opposing view and their evidence
Recognize that Shafi'i and Ahmad hold tawarruk in the last sitting, while Malik holds tawarruk in both sittings. Their evidence is the Prophet's own practice of tawarruk later in life.
3
Examine the Hanafi response
Understand that the Hanafi school interprets the Prophet's tawarruk as due to weakness and old age, not as a general sunnah to be emulated. They rely on the explicit prohibition of tawarruk reported by Anas.
The hadith: 'نهى عن التورك' (forbade tawarruk).
4
Determine the practical consequence
The difference affects how one sits during tashahhud. A Hanafi will always do ifirash; a Shafi'i will do ifirash in the first tashahhud and tawarruk in the last.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf.
5
Summarize the tarjih
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi (operative for fatwa) because the prohibition hadith is clear and the Prophet's later practice is explained away.
The authority weight: zahir al-riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وافتراش رجله اليسرى ونصب اليمنى: أي مطلقاً؛ لأنه عليه السلام كان إذا قعد فرش رجله اليسرى وقعد عليها ونصب رجله اليمنى، وعن أنس رضي الله عنه أن النبي عليه السلام نهى عن التورك، وهو أن يضع إليتيه على الأرض، ويخرج رجليه إلى الجانب الأيمن، وما احتج به الشافعي ومالك في توركه عليه السلام فمحمول على ضعفه وكبر سنه، وكذا يفترش بين السجدتين. (فتح، مستخلص) نصب اليمنى في حالة القعود للتشهد في القعدتين، وعند الشافعي وأحمد: يتورك في الآخرة، وعند مالك: يتورك فيهما. (ع)
And spreading his left foot and erecting the right: i.e., in all sittings; because he (peace be upon him) when he sat, he spread his left foot and sat on it and erected his right foot. And from Anas (may Allah be pleased with him) that the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade tawarruk, which is to place his buttocks on the ground and extend his legs to the right side. And what al-Shafi'i and Malik adduced regarding his tawarruk (peace be upon him) is interpreted as due to his weakness and old age. And similarly, he does ifirash between the two prostrations. (Fath, Mustakhlas) 'Erecting the right' in the state of sitting for tashahhud in both sittings. According to al-Shafi'i and Ahmad: he does tawarruk in the last sitting; according to Malik: he does tawarruk in both sittings. ('Ayni)
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage discusses the sunnah posture during the sitting positions in prayer, both between prostrations and for tashahhud. The Hanafi school holds that one should sit on the left foot (ifirash) with the right foot upright (nask). This applies to all sittings. The text reports a hadith from Anas that the Prophet forbade tawarruk, which is sitting on the buttocks with legs extended to the right. The Shafi'i and Maliki positions advocating tawarruk in the last or both sittings are countered by saying that the Prophet's own practice of tawarruk later in life was due to physical weakness and old age, not as a sunnah to be emulated. Therefore, the preferred Hanafi practice is ifirash throughout.

Key Terms
افتراش (ifirāsh): Sitting on the left foot with the right foot upright; the sunnah posture in all sittings according to Hanafi.
التورك (al-tawarruk): Sitting on the buttocks with legs extended to the right side.
نصب اليمنى (naṣb al-yumnā): Erecting the right foot vertically with toes pointing towards the qibla.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Ifirash in all sittings
C) Tawarruk in both sittings
D) No specific posture is sunnah

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds ifirash as the sunnah in all sittings based on a hadith forbidding tawarruk, and interprets the Prophet's later practice as due to weakness.

The Obligation of Qawma and Jalsa: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on qawma and jalsa with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the legal status of qawma and jalsa, and differentiate the views of Abu Yusuf, al-Karkhi, and Imam Muhammad, explaining the practical consequences of each.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school considers qawma and jalsa as sunnah, but Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i treat them as fard.

In the prayer, there are two brief pauses: the standing after ruku' (qawma) and the sitting between the two prostrations (jalsa). Are they obligatory or just recommended? This lesson explores the intra-Hanafi dispute and the Shafi'i position, highlighting how different rulings affect the validity of prayer.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the default Hanafi ruling
The matn (Kanz al-Daqaiq) lists qawma as the 20th sunnah and jalsa as the 21st sunnah, indicating the relied-upon view that they are sunnah mu'akkadah, not fard or wajib.
2
2. Contrast with Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i
Abu Yusuf (within the Hanafi school) and al-Shafi'i consider both acts fard, such that deliberately omitting them invalidates the prayer.
Abu Yusuf's view is a narration from the nawadir, not from zahir al-riwayah.
3
3. Consider al-Karkhi's narration
A narration from al-Karkhi regards them as wajib, which is a lower level than fard in Hanafi terminology, requiring sajdat al-sahw for forgetful omission.
Wajib in Hanafi usul is established by zanni evidence.
4
4. Trace the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if someone omits qawma or jalsa deliberately, under the sunnah view there is no effect; under the wajib view, sajdat al-sahw is needed; under the fard view, the prayer is invalid.
5
5. Identify the mufta bihi
The majority and the relied-upon view is sunnah, as stated in the matn and supported by Imam Muhammad. Some later scholars, however, considered omitting these sunnahs to require sajdat al-sahw as a precaution.
Refer to the Hanafi position box for tarjih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقومة والعشرون: القومة بين الركوع والسجود] وعند أبي يوسف والشافعي: القومة والجلسة فرضان حتى لو ترك القومة أو الجلسة فسدت صلاته، وفي رواية الكرخي : هما واجبان، وكذلك رفع الرأس من الركوع والانتصاب والطمأنينة، ولو ترك شيئا من ذلك ساهياً يلزمه سجود السهو. (فتح) والجلسة: والحادي والعشرون الجلسة بين السجدتين، وقال أبو يوسف والشافعي: هما فرض. (ع)
And al-qawma (the standing): the twentieth (sunnah): the qawma between ruku' and sujud. According to Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i, the qawma and jalsa are fard, such that if one abandons the qawma or jalsa, his prayer becomes invalid. According to a narration from al-Karkhi, they are wajib. And similarly, raising the head from ruku', standing straight, and tranquility (tuma'ninah). If one abandons any of that forgetfully, sajdat al-sahw becomes necessary. (Fath) And al-jalsa: the twenty-first (sunnah): the jalsa between the two prostrations. Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i said: both are fard. ('Ayni)
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage addresses the rulings of the brief standing (qawma) after ruku' and the brief sitting (jalsa) between prostrations. The matn lists them among the sunan. However, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute and an inter-madhhab dispute. Within the Hanafi school, Imam Abu Yusuf and the Shafi'i school consider both qawma and jalsa as fard (obligatory), such that deliberately omitting them invalidates the prayer. Another Hanafi narration from al-Karkhi considers them wajib (which is a level below fard in the Hanafi terminology), necessitating sajdat al-sahw for forgetful omission. The standard Hanafi position (Imam Muhammad and the relied-upon view) is that they are sunnah, but the text presents the alternative views. The text also mentions that raising the head from ruku', standing straight, and tranquility are related; the same rulings apply.

Key Terms
القومة (al-qawmah): The brief standing pause after rising from ruku' before going into sujud.
الجلسة (al-jalsah): The brief sitting pause between the two prostrations.
الطمأنينة (al-tuma'nīnah): Tranquility, stillness for a moment in each posture.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Fard (obligatory)
B) Wajib
C) Sunnah
D) Makruh

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's reliance on the majority view makes qawma and jalsa sunnah, not fard, but the existence of opposing views shows the scope of valid ijtihad.
